Drying and Perfuming the Deceased: Hanafi Practices
Covers drying the deceased, applying hanut and camphor, and the use of cotton.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah actions of drying and applying hanut and camphor to the deceased, and identify the disliked use of cotton according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Drying the deceased and applying hanut and camphor are sunnah; using cotton is makruh.

After washing the deceased, the body is dried and perfumed. The Hanafi school prescribes specific steps: drying with a clean cloth, applying hanut (compound perfume) to the head and beard, and camphor to the prostration points. In this lesson, we examine these practices and the difference of opinion regarding the inclusion of the nose and feet as prostration points, as well as the controversial use of cotton.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions after washing
Recognize that drying, applying hanut, and applying camphor are separate recommended acts.
These are sunnah, not obligatory.
2
Determine the ruling level for each action
The text states that perfuming (hanut and camphor) is sunnah. Drying is also sunnah by implication.
Sunnah means recommended; omission does not incur sin.
3
Locate the specific application sites
Hanut is placed on the head and beard; camphor on the prostration points (masajid) – the forehead, palms, knees, and feet (with difference on nose and feet).
The masajid are the seven points that touch the ground during prostration.
4
Examine the dispute on prostration points
Compare al-Sarakhsi (includes nose and feet) with al-Quduri (excludes them). The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is the seven points including the nose and feet? Actually both views exist; the majority includes them.
The commentator does not decisively settle the ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the use of cotton
Though a narration from Abu Hanifa permits cotton in nostrils and mouth, the majority of later scholars consider it makruh (disliked) because it is not in the apparent narrations.
This shows the principle of relying on zahir al-riwayah over isolated reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ونشف في ثوب كيلا يبتل أكفانه وفي الولواجية : المنديل الذي يمسح به الميت بعد الغسل كالمنديل الذي يمسح به الحي ا هـ . يعني أنه طاهر قوله : (وجعل الحنوط على رأسه ولحيته) لأن التطيب سنة . وذكر الرازي أن هذا الجعل مستحب والحنوط عطر مركب من أشياء طيبة ولا بأس بسائر الطيب غير الزعفران والورس اعتباراً بالحياة وقد ورد النهي عن المزعفر للرجال وبهذا يعلم جهل من يجعل الزعفران في الكفن عند رأس الميت في زماننا قوله : (والكافور على مساجده) زيادة في تكرمتها وصيانة للميت عن سرعة الفساد وهي موضع سجوده جمع مسجد بالفتح لا غير كذا في المغرب. واختلف فيها فذكر السرخسي أنها الجبهة والأنف واليدان والركبتان والقدمان. وذكره القدوري في شرح الكرخي أنها الجبهة واليدان والركبتان ولم يذكر الأنف والقدمين. كذا في غاية البيان. ولم يذكر المصنف في الغسل استعمال القطن لأنه لم يرد في الروايات الظاهرة. وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يجعل القطن المحلوج في منخريه وفمه. وقال بعضهم : في صماخيه وقال بعضهم : في دبره أيضاً. قال في الظهيرية : واستقبحه عامة المشايخ
His statement: 'and he dries him with a cloth so that his shroud does not become wet.' And in al-Walwajiyyah: the towel used to wipe the deceased after washing is like the towel used to wipe the living—meaning it is pure. His statement: 'and he places hanut on his head and beard' because perfuming is sunnah. Al-Razi stated that this placing is recommended. Hanut is a compound perfume made from pleasant things. There is no harm in other perfumes besides saffron and wars, by analogy with life. And the prohibition of saffron for men is reported. By this, the ignorance of those who put saffron in the shroud at the head of the deceased in our time becomes known. His statement: 'and camphor on his prostration points' is an extra honor and preservation of the deceased from rapid decay. These are the places of prostration: the plural of masjid (prostration point) with fatha only, as in al-Maghrib. There is difference of opinion: al-Sarakhsi said they are the forehead, nose, palms, knees, and feet. Al-Quduri in his commentary on al-Karkhi said they are the forehead, palms, and knees, and did not include the nose and feet. Similarly in Ghayat al-Bayan. The author did not mention using cotton in the washing because it is not found in the apparent narrations. From Abu Hanifa, it is reported that he places raw cotton in his nostrils and mouth. Some said in his ear canals, and some said in his anus as well. In al-Zahiriyyah: the majority of shaykhs considered this disliked.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage covers the steps after washing the deceased. First, the body is dried with a clean towel to prevent the shroud from getting wet. Then, hanut (a compound perfume) is applied to the head and beard, and camphor is placed on the prostration points (the seven points of the body that touch the ground during prostration: forehead, nose, hands, knees, feet). There is a difference of opinion on whether the nose and feet are included, with al-Sarakhsi including them and al-Quduri excluding. The author also discusses the use of cotton: although some narrations from Abu Hanifa suggest placing cotton in the nostrils, mouth, and even the anus, the majority of scholars consider this disliked (makruh) because it is not proven in the apparent narrations (zahir al-riwayah). Saffron is prohibited for men's clothing and shrouds.

Key Terms
حنوط (hanut): A compound perfume used for the deceased
كافور (kafur): Camphor, used as a preservative and perfume
مساجد (masajid (plural of masjid)): Prostration points of the body (forehead, nose, palms, knees, feet)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Forehead
B) Nose
C) Palms
D) Elbows

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, drying the deceased and applying hanut and camphor are sunnah; using cotton is makruh.

Prohibition of Combing and Cutting the Deceased's Hair, Beard, and Nails
Ruling statement on prohibited acts for the deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on combing the hair, beard, and cutting nails of the deceased, identify the rationale, and apply the exception for a broken nail.

Core Idea
Combing and cutting the deceased's hair/nails is prohibited as adornment, but repair of a broken nail is allowed.

When handling a deceased body, certain actions are restricted because the deceased is beyond worldly adornment. This passage clarifies that combing hair, beard, and cutting nails is not permissible, but a broken nail may be trimmed. Understanding this distinction prevents unnecessary acts and ensures proper respect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text explicitly prohibits combing the head hair and beard, and cutting nails. The default ruling is 'not permissible' (haram).
The passage includes 'beard' with 'hair' for emphasis.
2
Note the rationale
These acts are for adornment (zinah) of the living. The deceased no longer needs adornment, so the actions are meaningless and prohibited.
Perfume is allowed because it serves a different purpose (cleansing/odor removal).
3
Identify the exception
If a nail breaks, there is no harm in trimming it to prevent harm or further breakage. This is reported from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The exception is limited to necessary repair, not cosmetic cutting.
4
Apply to similar acts
Any act intended solely for adornment (e.g., applying makeup to the face) would likely fall under the same prohibition. Acts for preservation or preparation (e.g., closing the mouth, cleaning) are separate.
The underlying principle: the deceased is not beautified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يسرح شعره ولحيته ولا يقص ظفره وشعره لأنها للزينة وقد استغنى عنها، والظاهر أن هذا الصنيع لا يجوز . قال في القنية : أما التزين بعد موتها والامتشاط وقطع الشعر لا يجوز والطيب يجوز والأصح أنه يجوز للزوج أن يراها. وفي المجتبى : ولا بأس بتقبيل الميت. وذكر اللحية مع الشعر من باب عطف الجزء على الكل اهتماماً . بمنع تسريحها، وليس هو من قبيل التكرار كما توهمه الشارح وفي الظهيرية ولو تكسر ظفر الميت فلا بأس بأن يؤخذ، روي ذلك عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ا.هـ.
His statement: 'and he does not comb his hair or beard, nor cut his nails or hair because they are for adornment and he is no longer in need of adornment. And apparently this action is not permissible.' In al-Qunyah: 'As for adornment after death, combing and cutting hair are not permissible, but perfume is permissible. The correct view is that it is permissible for the husband to look at her.' In al-Mujtaba: 'There is no harm in kissing the deceased.' Mentioning the beard along with hair is a matter of including the part with the whole for emphasis on prohibiting combing it, and it is not repetition as the commentator thought. In al-Zahiriyyah: 'If the deceased's nail breaks, there is no harm in trimming it, reported from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage explains that combing the hair or beard of the deceased, as well as cutting nails, is not permissible. The reason given is that these acts are for adornment, and the deceased has no need for adornment. However, perfume is allowed. The author notes that the prohibition includes the beard along with hair as a part-whole relationship for emphasis. An exception is made: if a nail breaks, it may be trimmed, as reported from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The passage also mentions that it is permissible for the husband to look at his deceased wife, and there is no harm in kissing the deceased.

Key Terms
تسريح (tasrih): Combing or grooming hair
زينة (zinah): Adornment or beautification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on combing the deceased's beard?
A) Sunnah
B) Permissible
C) Not permissible
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
Combing hair, beard, and cutting nails of the deceased is prohibited because it is adornment; only trimming a broken nail is allowed.

The Collective Obligation of Washing the Deceased
Prose compilation of rulings on washing the deceased.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that washing the deceased is fard kifayah and explain the consequences of abandoning it or performing prayer before washing.

Core Idea
Washing the deceased is a communal duty; if abandoned, the community is fought.

Washing the deceased is one of the essential obligations that the Muslim community must fulfill. This lesson explains its legal classification as a communal obligation (fard kifayah), what happens if the entire community neglects it, and how the prayer relates to washing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation status
Recognize that washing the deceased is fard kifayah—a collective duty. If some perform it, the rest are absolved; if all abandon it, they are sinful and the authorities fight them.
2
Understand the consequence of abandonment
Note that if a town's inhabitants collectively leave the washing, they are fought. This underscores the seriousness of the obligation.
3
Determine the effect of prayer before washing
If the funeral prayer is performed before washing, it must be repeated after washing. This shows that washing is a prerequisite for a valid prayer.
4
Note the exception for partial remains after burial
If a body part is found after burial but before soil is thrown, the body is exhumed, washed, and prayer repeated. If soil has been thrown, no exhumation. This limits disruption.
5
Review related rulings on expenses and non-liability
The passage also touches on expenses from the estate and that accidental water (rain) does not count as washing. These support the rule that washing is a deliberate act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف صفة الغسل ومن يغسل والغاسل وحكم الميت قبله وبعده. أما الأول فهو من فروض الكفاية كالصلاة عليه وتجهيزه ودفنه حتى لو اجتمع أهل بلدة على تركها ،قوتلوا ولو صلوا عليه قبل الغسل أعادوا الصلاة. وكذا إذا ذكروا قبل أن يهال عليه التراب ينزع اللبن ويخرج ويغسل ويصلي عليه، وإن أهالوه لم ينبش ولم تعد الصلاة عليه، ولو بقي منه عضو فذكروه بعد الصلاة والتكفين يغسل ذلك العضو ويعاد، فإن بقي أصبع ونحوه بعد التكفين لا يغسل. وقال محمد: يغسل على كل حال كذا في المجتبى. وفي القنية : وجد رأس آدمي لا يغسل ولا يصلى عليه ولو غسل صار الماء مستعملاً، ولو مات في بيته فقالت الورثة لا نرضى بغسله فيه ليس لهم ذلك لأن غسله في بيته من حوائجه وهي مقدمة على الورثة ا.هـ. وفي الظهيرية : والأفضل أن يغسل الميت مجاناً فإن ابتغى الغاسل الأجر فهو على وجهين : إن كان هناك غيره يجوز أخذ الأجر وإلا فلا. واختلفوا في استئجاء الخياط لخياطة الكفن وأجرة الحاملين والحفار والدفان من رأس المال ا هـ . وفي الخانية : إذا جرى الماء على الميت أو أصابه المطر عن أبي يوسف أنه لا ينوب عن الغسل لأنا أمرنا بالغسل وجريان الماء وإصابة المطر ليس بغسل. والغريق يغسل ثلاثاً عند أبي يوسف. وعن محمد: إذا نوى الغسل عند الإخراج من الماء يغسل مرتين وإن لم ينو يغسل ثلاثاً، وفي رواية يغسل مرة واحدة ا هـ . وفي فتح القدير : الظاهر اشتراط النية فيه لإسقاط وجوبه عن المكلف لا لتحصيل طهارته هو وشرط صحة الصلاة عليه ا هـ. وفي فتاوی قاضيخان ميت غسله أهله بغير نية أجزأهم ذلك اهـ. واختاره في الغاية : قوله : (وفي رواية يغسل مرة واحدة قال الرملي قال في الفتح كأن هذه الرواية ذكر فيها القدر الواجب قوله : ( وفي فتاوى قاضيخان ميت غسله أهله الخ كان نكتة ذكره بعد كلام الفتح
The author did not mention the description of washing, who washes, the washers, and the ruling on the deceased before and after. As for the first (the description), it is fard kifayah like praying over him, shrouding him, and burying him; so much so that if the inhabitants of a town collectively abandon it, they are fought. If they pray over him before washing, they repeat the prayer. Similarly, if they do mention before the burial soil is thrown, the brick is removed, he is taken out, washed, and prayed over. If they have already thrown soil, he is not exhumed and the prayer is not repeated. If a limb remains and they mention it after prayer and shrouding, that limb is washed and the prayer is repeated. If a finger or similar remains after shrouding, it is not washed. Muhammad said: it is washed in all cases—as in al-Mujtaba. In al-Qunyah: if a human head is found, it is not washed nor prayed over, and if washed, the water becomes used. If someone dies in his house and the heirs say we are not pleased with washing him in the house, they do not have that right because washing him in the house is one of his needs, and that takes precedence over the heirs. In al-Zahiriyyah: the best is that the deceased be washed for free. If the washer seeks payment, there are two aspects: if there is someone else, taking payment is permissible; otherwise not. They differed over hiring a tailor for stitching the shroud and the wages of carriers, diggers, and buriers from the capital. In al-Khaniyyah: if water runs over the deceased or rain hits him, from Abu Yusuf: that does not suffice for washing because we are commanded to wash, and running water or rain is not washing. The drowned person is washed three times according to Abu Yusuf. From Muhammad: if he intends ghusl upon removal from water, he washes twice; if he does not intend, he washes three times; and in one narration, once. In Fath al-Qadir: apparently, intention is required to remove the obligation from the responsible person, not to achieve his purification, and it is a condition for the validity of prayer over him. In Fatawa Qadikhan: if the family of a deceased washes him without intention, it is sufficient for them. And al-Ghayyah chose this.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage collects various rulings that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not mention. First, washing the deceased is a communal obligation (fard kifayah); if a community abandons it, they are fought. If prayer is performed before washing, it must be repeated. If a body part is found after burial, it is washed and the prayer repeated only if the burial soil has not yet been thrown; otherwise, no exhumation. If a limb remains after shrouding, it is washed and prayer repeated, but if it is a finger or smaller, it is not washed (though Muhammad says it is always washed). Other rulings: a found head is not washed or prayed over; washing in the deceased's own home takes precedence over heirs' wishes; the washer should ideally do it for free, but can charge if no one else is available; expenses of tailoring, carrying, digging, and rebutting come from the estate. Intention is required for the obligation to be fulfilled, though Fatawa Qadikhan says family washing without intention suffices.

Key Terms
فرض كفاية (fard kifayah): A collective obligation; if some perform it, the rest are absolved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a town's entire population abandons washing a deceased Muslim, what should be done?
A) They are fine as it is not obligatory
B) They are fought
C) Only the imam is blamed
D) The deceased is buried as is

Key Takeaway
Washing the deceased is a collective obligation; negligence leads to sin and the community is fought.

Intention and Special Cases in Washing the Deceased
Prose compilation of rulings on intention and special cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi positions on intention for washing, the status of a found head, and the ruling on washing a limb after shrouding.

Core Idea
Intention is required for validity, but family washing without intention suffices.

The washing of the deceased involves details about intention, partial remains, and special circumstances like drowning. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi disputes and clarifications on these points.

Guided Path
1
Idenify the dispute on intention
Fath al-Qadir states that intention is required to remove the obligation from the community; Qadikhan says family washing without intention suffices. Understand the two positions.
2
Apply the ruling to a found head
A severed human head is not washed nor prayed over; washing it would make the water used (musta'mal). Recognize this exception.
3
Apply the ruling to a limb after shrouding
If a limb (other than a small part like a finger) is found after shrouding and prayer, it is washed and the prayer repeated. Muhammad says even a finger must be washed.
4
Note the drowned person case
Abu Yusuf says the drowned person is washed three times; Muhammad requires intention upon removal from water for the number of washes.
5
Review the ruling on rain water
Accidental water (rain or running water) does not count as washing; intentional washing is required.

intermediate
7 min
Intention for Washing the Deceased: Obligation or Reward
Critical refinement on intention in washing the deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intention for washing the deceased and explain why intention is required for reward but not for discharging the communal obligation.

Core Idea
The act itself suffices; intention is needed for reward, not to fulfill the obligation.

A common question in funeral rites is whether intention (niyyah) is necessary for washing the deceased. Some scholars insist it is required for validity. In this lesson, we examine the relied-upon Hanafi view that intention is crucial for gaining reward but not for removing the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The debate centers on whether intention is necessary for washing the deceased to discharge the fard kifayah obligation.
The primary source is a dispute among later Hanafi scholars.
2
State the Hanafi position
The majority (al-Ghaya, al-Aspijabi, al-Halabi) hold that the act itself suffices; intention is only for reward.
They rely on analogy with washing the living and the story of Hanzala.
3
Examine the opposing view
Some later scholars (e.g., authors of al-Fath) argued that intention is necessary to lift the obligation, likening washing to other acts of worship.
This view is also cited in Fatawa Qadi Khan.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The obligation here is merely the action of washing; it is not an act of worship per se. Intention is only required for reward, as proven by the validity of a dhimmi washing her husband.
The usul principle: 'what is obligatory for other than itself, its existence suffices, not its intentional performance.'
5
Determine the practical consequence
If someone washes the deceased without intention, the obligation is still discharged, but they do not receive the reward of worship.
Thus, for communal obligation, the action itself is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي رواية يغسل مرة واحدة قال الرملي قال في الفتح كأن هذه الرواية ذكر فيها القدر الواجب قوله : ( وفي فتاوى قاضيخان ميت غسله أهله الخ كان نكتة ذكره بعد كلام الفتح
كتاب الجنائز ٣٠٥
والأسبيجابي لأن غسل الحي لا يشترط له النية فكذا غسل الميت. وأما الثاني فالموتى ضربان : من يغسل ومن لا يغسل . والأول ضربان من يغسل ليصلى عليه ومن يغسل لا للصلاة. فالأول من مات بعد الولادة وله حكم الإسلام والثاني الجنين الميت على ما سيأتي، وكذا الكافر غير الحربي إذا مات وله ولي مسلم كما سيأتي. والثاني ضربان : من لا يغسل إهانة وعقوبة كقتلى أهل البغي والحرب وقطاع الطريق وضرب لا يغسل إكراماً وفضيلة كالشهداء ولو اختلط موتى المسلمين بموتى الكفار يغسلون إن كان المسلمون أكثر وإلا فلا. ومن يدري أمسلم أم كافر إن كان عليه سيما المسلمين أو في بقاع ديار الإسلام يغسل وإلا فلا . ولو وجد الأكثر من الميت أو النصف مع الرأس غسل وصلى عليه وإلا فلا.
وأما الغاسل فمن شرطه أن يحل له النظر إلى المغسول فلا يغسل الرجل المرأة ولا المرأة الرجل والمجبوب والخصي فأما الخنثى المشكل المراهق إذا مات ففيه اختلاف والظاهر أنه ييمم وإذا ماتت المرأة في السفر بين الرجال ييممها ذو رحم محرم منها، وإن لم يكن لف الأجنبي على يديه خرقة ثم ييممها، وإن كانت أمة ييممها الأجنبي بغير ثوب، وكذا إذا مات رجل بين النساء تيممه ذات محرم منه أو زوجته أو أمته بغير ثوب وغيرهن بثوب، ا هـ.
غسله الإشارة إلى أن قول قاضيخان أجزأهم وإطلاق عدم الاشتراط المنقول عن الغاية والأسبيجابي ربما يخالف ما ذكره تأمل . ثم رأيت الحلبي في شرح المنية بحث مع الفتح بما حاصله أن ما مر عن محمد وعن أبي يوسف يفيد أن الفرض فعل الغسل له منا حتى لو غسله لتعليم الغير كفي، وليس فيه ما يفيد اشتراط النية لإسقاط الوجوب بحيث يستحق العقاب بتركها، وقد تقرر في الأصول ما وجب لغيره من الأفعال الحسية يشترط وجوده لا إيجاده كالسعي والطهارة نعم لا ينال ثواب العبادة بدونها ونقل كلامه الباقاني وأقره عليه وأيده بما في المحيط : لو وجد الميت في الماء لا بد من لأن الخطاب يتوجه إلى بني آدم ولم يوجد منهم فعل ا هـ . فالحاصل أنه لا بد في إسقاط الواجب من الفعل، وأما النية فشرط لتحصيل الثواب ولذا صح تغسيل الذمية زوحها كما سيأتي مع أن النية من شروطها الإسلام، فظهر أن ما استظهره في الفتح غير ظاهر بل الظاهر ما جزم به في الخانية واختاره في الغاية والأسبيجابي. ثم الظاهر أيضاً أن الشرط حصول الفعل، سواء كان من المكلف أو لا، بدليل قصة حنظلة غسيل الملائكة رضي الله تعالى عنه. وعلى هذا فالظاهر سقوط الواجب بفعل صبي يعقل أيضاً كما يسقط عن المكلفين رد السلام بفعله إذا سلم عليهم رجل وفيهم صبي فرد السلام، وكما تصح ذبيحته مع أن شرط حلها التسمية فهو أهل لفعل الواجب في الجملة، وكذا ينبغي أن يسقط الوجوب بحمله الميت ودفنه. وقال في الأشباه والنظائر في أحكام الصبيان: وأما فرض الكفاية فهل يسقط بفعله فقالوا يسقط . كذا في بعض نسخ الأشباه وفي بعضها فقالوا لا ويؤيد النسخة الأولى ما قدمناه .
His statement: (And in a narration, he is washed once. Al-Ramli said: In al-Fath it is as if this narration mentions the obligatory amount. His statement: (And in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan: a deceased washed by his family... was a point mentioned after the words of al-Fath. Kitab al-Jana'iz 305. And al-Aspijabi because the washing of the living does not require intention, so too the washing of the deceased. As for the second, the deceased are of two types: those who are washed and those who are not. And the first are two types: those washed to be prayed over and those washed not for prayer. The first is one who dies after birth and has the ruling of Islam; the second is the dead fetus as will come, and likewise the non-hostile infidel when he dies and has a Muslim guardian as will come. And the second are two types: those not washed as humiliation and punishment, like the slain of rebels and combatants and highway robbers, and a type not washed out of honor and virtue, like martyrs. And if the dead of Muslims are mixed with the dead of infidels, they are washed if the Muslims are more numerous; otherwise not. And whoever it is not known whether Muslim or infidel, if he has the signs of Muslims or is in the districts of the abode of Islam, he is washed; otherwise not. And if the majority of the body or half with the head is found, he is washed and prayed over; otherwise not. As for the washer, one of his conditions is that it is permissible for him to look at the one being washed. So a man does not wash a woman, nor a woman a man, nor a eunuch or hermaphrodite. As for the ambiguous pubescent hermaphrodite when he dies, there is a difference of opinion, and apparently he is to be performed tayammum upon. And if a woman dies in travel among men, a mahram relative performs tayammum on her; if there is none, a stranger wraps a cloth on his hands and then performs tayammum on her; and if she is a slave, a stranger performs tayammum on her without a cloth. Likewise, if a man dies among women, a mahram, his wife, or his slave performs tayammum on him without a cloth, and others with a cloth, end. [The discussion continues:] The statement that Qadi Khan said 'it suffices them' and the absolute lack of condition transmitted from al-Ghaya and al-Aspijabi may contradict what he mentioned, ponder. Then I saw al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munya discuss with al-Fath, the summary being that what passed from Muhammad and from Abu Yusuf indicates that the obligation is the act of washing itself from us, even if he washed him for teaching someone else it suffices, and there is nothing in it indicating the requirement of intention to remove the obligation such that he deserves punishment for leaving it. And it has been established in usul that what is obligatory for other than itself among physical actions, its existence is required, not its intentional performance, like walking and purification. However, one cannot attain the reward of worship without it. And he transmitted al-Baqqani's words and approved of him and supported him with what is in al-Muhit: If the deceased is found in water, there is no need because the address is directed to human beings and no action from them occurred. So the conclusion is that to remove the obligation, the action is necessary; as for intention, it is a condition for attaining reward. Thus, the washing of a dhimmi of her husband is valid, as will come, even though intention is among its conditions and Islam. So it becomes clear that what al-Fath considered apparent is not apparent; rather the apparent is what was asserted in al-Khaniya and chosen by al-Ghaya and al-Aspijabi. Then it is also apparent that the condition is the occurrence of the action, whether from the obligated person or not, as evidenced by the story of Hanzala, who was washed by the angels. Upon this, it is apparent that the obligation is removed by the action of a discerning child, just as the obligation of responding to salam is removed from the obligated persons if a man greets them and there is a child among them and the child responds, and as his slaughter is valid even though the condition for its permissibility is the basmala, so he is qualified to perform the obligation in general. Likewise, it is suitable that the obligation is removed by carrying the deceased and burying him. And it is said in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir regarding the rulings of children: As for fard kifayah, does it become discharged by his action? They said it does. This is found in some copies of al-Ashbah, and in some they said it does not, and the first copy is supported by what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the debate on whether intention (niyyah) is required for washing the deceased, followed by a classification of the deceased and conditions for the washer. The discussion begins with a comment on a narration that washing once suffices, referencing al-Fath and Qadi Khan. Al-Aspijabi argues that just as living washing does not require intention, so too the deceased. The text then categorizes the deceased: those washed for prayer (Muslims who died after birth, dead fetuses, non-hostile kafirs with Muslim guardians), those washed but not for prayer (martyrs, rebels, etc.). It also covers mixing of Muslim and kafir bodies, and unidentified bodies. Then it discusses the washer: a man cannot wash a woman or vice versa; ambiguous hermaphrodites are washed by tayammum; travel cases are detailed. Finally, it returns to intention: the relied-upon view is that intention is not required for the obligation to be discharged; the act itself suffices, as shown by the story of Hanzala (washed by angels) and by analogy with a child's actions. Al-Halabi supports this, concluding that fard kifayah can be discharged by a discerning child.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention; in this context, the conscious intent to perform the ritual washing of the deceased.
غاسل (ghasil): The person who performs the washing of the deceased.
فرض كفاية (fard kifayah): A communal obligation; if some members perform it, the obligation is discharged for all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the role of intention (niyyah) in washing the deceased?
A) It is an absolute requirement for the validity of washing.
B) It is required only for the person washing to receive reward, not for discharging the obligation.
C) It is not required at all, neither for obligation nor reward.
D) It is required only for the community, not the individual.

Key Takeaway
The act of washing the deceased discharges the communal obligation even without intention; intention is only for reward.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
8 min
Classification of the Deceased and Conditions of the Washer
Systematic classification of deceased and washer conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the categories of deceased who are washed and not washed, and state the conditions for the washer.

Core Idea
The deceased fall into categories based on eligibility for washing; the washer must be permissible to see the body.

Just as intention is debated, the identity of the deceased and the washer matters. Some deceased are washed for prayer, some for honor, some not at all. The washer’s relationship to the deceased determines who can perform the washing. This lesson systematizes these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of deceased
Deceased are divided into those who are washed and those who are not. Then subcategories based on reason.
This is a classification exercise.
2
List those who are washed for prayer
Include Muslims who died after birth, dead fetus (with certain conditions), non-hostile kafir with Muslim guardian.
These are washed to enable prayer over them.
3
List those washed without prayer
Include martyrs (washed out of honor but not prayed over according to some views) and others like rebels and highway robbers who are not washed at all.
Martyrs are an exception: they are not washed, but buried in their clothes.
4
Handle mixed or unknown bodies
If Muslim and kafir bodies are mixed, wash if Muslims are majority; if unknown, look for signs (circumcision, Muslim clothing) or location (Muslim land).
Also, if only part of a body is found, wash if majority or half with head.
5
State the conditions of the washer
The washer must be permitted to see the deceased’s body. This restricts opposite genders, and special rules for hermaphrodites and travel situations.
In travel, if no mahram, use tayammum with barrier.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the debate on whether intention (niyyah) is required for washing the deceased, followed by a classification of the deceased and conditions for the washer. The discussion begins with a comment on a narration that washing once suffices, referencing al-Fath and Qadi Khan. Al-Aspijabi argues that just as living washing does not require intention, so too the deceased. The text then categorizes the deceased: those washed for prayer (Muslims who died after birth, dead fetuses, non-hostile kafirs with Muslim guardians), those washed but not for prayer (martyrs, rebels, etc.). Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): Martyr; one who dies in battle for Islam or other specific causes. They are not washed but buried in their clothes.
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): A person with ambiguous genitalia whose sex is unclear.
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently forbidden, such as a father, brother, uncle, etc.

Which of the following deceased is NOT washed according to the passage?
A) A Muslim who died after birth.

Key Takeaway
The deceased are classified into those who must be washed, those washed for prayer, and those not washed; the washer must be permissible to see the body.

Conditions of the Washer and Tayammum for the Deceased in Travel
Sets conditions for who can wash the deceased and rules for tayammum when washing is not possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for who may wash the deceased and apply the rules for tayammum when washing is not possible.

Core Idea
A man cannot wash a woman and vice versa; when water is impossible, tayammum is performed with a barrier.

This passage sets out the fundamental rules for washing the deceased, focusing on who may perform the washing and what to do when washing with water is not feasible. Understanding these conditions ensures the janazah is handled with the proper legal respect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the washer
Recognize that the key condition is that the washer must be legally permitted to see the uncovered body of the deceased (i.e., it must be halal for them to look).
This is based on the prohibition of looking at the 'awrah of a non-mahram.
2
Identify prohibited pairings
Note that a man cannot wash a woman, a woman cannot wash a man, and eunuchs or castrated men are also prohibited from washing women.
The prohibition is due to the possibility of desire, ruling out all non-mahram opposite genders.
3
Learn the ruling for the khuntha mushkil
Understand that for a hermaphrodite with unclear gender who has reached puberty, there is a difference of opinion. The apparent position treats it as male for washing purposes.
The text is incomplete but indicates ikhtilaf.
4
Identify tayammum rules in travel
If a woman dies among men, a mahram relative performs tayammum. If no mahram exists, a non-mahram man wraps a cloth on his hands and then performs tayammum. For a slave woman, a non-mahram can do it without a cloth.
The cloth barrier prevents direct contact with the 'awrah.
5
Apply the reverse scenario for a man
If a man dies among women, a mahram woman, his wife, or his slave woman performs tayammum without a cloth; other women do so with a cloth.
The same principle of necessity applies.

Source Text

وأما الغاسل فمن شرطه أن يحل له النظر إلى المغسول فلا يغسل الرجل المرأة ولا المرأة الرجل والمجبوب والخصي فأما الخنثى المشكل المراهق إذا مات ففيه اختلاف والظاهر أنه وإذا ماتت المرأة في السفر بين الرجال ييممها ذو رحم محرم منها، وإن لم يكن لف الأجنبي على يديه خرقة ثم ييممها، وإن كانت أمة ييممها الأجنبي بغير ثوب، وكذا إذا مات رجل بين النساء تيممه ذات محرم منه أو زوجته أو أمته بغير ثوب وغيرهن بثوت، ييمم اهـ.
As for the washer, his condition is that it is permissible for him to look at the one being washed. So a man may not wash a woman, nor a woman a man, nor a eunuch or castrated man. As for an ambiguous hermaphrodite who has reached puberty, if he dies, there is difference of opinion, and the apparent is... And if a woman dies in travel among men, a mahram relative performs tayammum for her. If there is none, a non-mahram man wraps a cloth on his hands and then performs tayammum. If she is a slave, a non-mahram performs tayammum without a cloth. Similarly, if a man dies among women, a mahram woman or his wife or his slave woman performs tayammum for him without a cloth, and others with a cloth.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage states the conditions for the person who washes the deceased (ghasil). The key condition is that it must be lawful for the washer to see the uncovered body of the deceased. Therefore, a man cannot wash a woman (and vice versa), and a eunuch (majbub) or castrated man (khasi) cannot wash a woman because they are still male. For a hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) who has reached puberty, there is a difference of opinion; the apparent position (al-zahir) leans toward... (the text is incomplete). When a woman dies in travel and only men are present, tayammum (dry ablution) is performed for her by a mahram relative (unmarriageable kin). If no mahram is present, a non-mahram man wraps a cloth on his hands and performs tayammum. If the woman is a slave, a non-mahram can perform tayammum without a cloth because the slave is not as protected. The same rules apply in reverse for a man dying among women. This ruling is based on the principle that direct contact with the deceased's 'awrah is prohibited except for mahram or with a barrier. It is a rule of necessity (darura).

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A relative whom one cannot marry due to blood, marriage, or fosterage.
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): A person with ambiguous genitalia whose gender cannot be determined.
غاسل (ghasil): The person who washes the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a situation where a woman dies in travel with only non-mahram men present, how is tayammum performed?
A) A non-mahram man performs tayammum directly with his hands
B) A non-mahram man wraps a cloth on his hands and then performs tayammum
C) A mahram relative is not required; anyone can do it
D) Tayammum is not allowed; they must carry the body to a place with women

Key Takeaway
The deceased must be washed by someone lawfully permitted to see their body; when water is unavailable, tayammum is performed with a barrier if necessary.

Who May Wash the Deceased: Conditions for Spouses and Others
States who washes children and detailed rules for spouses washing each other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on who may wash a deceased Muslim and apply the condition of marital permissibility to various scenarios.

Core Idea
A wife may wash her husband, but a husband may never wash his wife, even after divorce during iddah.

This lesson covers the detailed Hanafi rulings on who is eligible to wash a deceased person, focusing on children and spouses. The primary rule is that a husband cannot wash his wife, but a wife can wash her husband provided the marriage bond exists at the time of washing. Several exceptions and special cases follow, including divorced wives, apostates, slaves, and non-Muslims. Understanding these conditions ensures that the burial rites are performed correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule for Children
Boys and girls who have not reached the age of desire (before puberty) can be washed by either men or women because they have no 'awrah.
This is a simplification based on the lack of sexual attraction.
2
State the Spouse Rule and Its Condition
A husband does not wash his wife, but a wife may wash her husband. The key condition is that the marriage must be valid and exist at the moment of washing.
Consummation is irrelevant; the condition is ongoing marriage.
3
List Exceptions to the Wife's Permission
The wife cannot wash if she is irrevocably divorced (ba'in) and in iddah, apostate, related by fosterage or affinity, or if there is a shubha situation. Also, a Magian wife cannot wash.
These all negate the marital permissibility (hilliyyah).
4
Handle Special Cases: Master-Slave, Ambiguous Divorce, Dhimmis
A master cannot wash his umm walad, mudabbara, or mukataba. In case of ambiguous divorce, both wives are prevented. A dhimmi who knows proper washing can perform it.
The underlying principle is that the permissibility of touch during life extends to after death.
5
Apply the Underlying Principle
The rulings are based on whether the deceased and washer were permitted to touch each other during life. If yes, washing is allowed; if not, it is prohibited.
This explains the exception for zihar (permitted) versus Magian (not permitted).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصبي الذي لا يشتهى والصبية كذلك غسلهما الرجال والنساء، ولا يغسل الرجل زوجته والزوجة تغسل زوجها دخل بها أو لا بشرط بقاء الزوجية عند الغسل حتى لو كانت مبانة بالطلاق وهي في العدة أو محرمة بردة أو رضاع أو مصاهرة لم تغسله، ولم يغسل المولى أم ولده وكذا مدبرته ومكاتبته وكذا على العكس في المشهور عن أبي حنيفة. الكل في المجتبى . وفي الواقعات : رجل له امرأتان قال إحداكما طالق ثلاثاً بعد الدخول بهما ثم مات قبل أن يبين فليس لواحدة منهما أن تغسله الجواز أن كل واحدة منهما ،مطلقة ولهما الميراث وعليهما عدة الطلاق والوفاة. ولو مات عن امرأته وهي مجوسية لم تغسله لأنه كان لا يحل له المس حال حياته فكذا بعد وفاته بخلاف التي ظاهر منها لأن الحل قائم، فإن أسلمت قبل أن يغسل غسلته اعتباراً بحالة الحياة. وكذا لو مات عن امرأته وأختها منه في عدته لم تغسله فإن انقضت عدتها قبل أن يغسل غسلته لما قلنا ا هـ . وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتدت المنكوحة بعد موته أو قبلت ابنه لا تغسله وكذا إذا وطئت بالشبهة لأن هذه الأشياء تنافي النكاح وتحرم المس. وفيها : إذا كان مع النساء رجل من أهل الذمة أو مع الرحال امرأة ذمية يعلمان الغسل لأن السنة تتأدى بغسله ولكن لا يهتدى إلى السنة فيعلم. وفي المحيط : لو مات عنها وهي حامل فوضعت لا تغسله لانقضاء عدتها.
And the boy who does not desire and the girl likewise, both men and women wash them. A man does not wash his wife, but the wife washes her husband, whether consummated or not, provided the marriage bond exists at the time of washing. So if she is separated by divorce and in her waiting period, or forbidden by apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, she does not wash him. The master does not wash his umm walad, nor his mudabbara, nor his mukataba, and likewise the reverse according to the famous view of Abu Hanifah. All in al-Mujtaba. And in al-Waqi'at: A man has two wives; he says 'one of you is thrice divorced' after consummation with both, then dies before specifying. Neither may wash him, as each is potentially divorced. Yet both inherit and observe both the divorce and death waiting periods. And if he dies leaving his wife who is a Magian, she does not wash him, because it was not permissible for him to touch her during life, so likewise after death. Unlike the wife from whom he performed zihar, because permissibility remains. If she accepts Islam before he is washed, she washes him, considering the state of life. Similarly, if he dies leaving his wife and her sister in her waiting period from him, she does not wash him. But if her waiting period expires before he is washed, she washes him, for what we said. In al-Walwaljiyya: If the wedded wife apostatizes after his death or breastfeeds his son, she does not wash him. Similarly, if she is penetrated by mistake (shubha), because these things negate marriage and prohibit touching. In it: If among women there is a dhimmi man, or among men a dhimmi woman, who knows the washing, it is sufficient, because the sunna is fulfilled by their washing, but they are not guided to the sunna, so they are taught. In al-Muhit: If a wife dies while pregnant and gives birth, she does not wash him due to the expiry of her waiting period.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part ruling set from the matn and later authoritative works. First, children who have not reached the age of desire (i.e., before puberty, typically before 10) can be washed by either men or women because they have no ‘awrah. Second, spouses: a husband does not wash his wife, but a wife may wash her husband regardless of consummation, provided the marriage is still valid at the moment of washing. Several exception cases follow: (1) If the wife is irrevocably divorced (ba'in) but still in ‘iddah, she cannot wash him because the marriage bond is broken. (2) If she becomes a murtaddah (apostate), or there is fosterage or affinity, the prohibition remains. (3) The master (mawla) cannot wash his umm walad (slave who bore his child), mudabbara (promised freedom after death), or mukataba (slave under contract), and vice versa. This is the famous view of Abu Hanifah. (4) A man with two wives who ambiguously divorces one then dies: both are potential divorcees and cannot wash him. (5) A Magian wife: she cannot wash him because interfaith marriage is invalid in Hanafi law (since a Muslim man cannot marry a Magian). If she converts before he is washed, she may wash him. (6) If the wife commits zihar (declaring her like his mother's back) but then resumes relations, she remains permissible to wash. (7) If the wife is in ‘iddah after a shubha intercourse with her sister, she cannot wash him until her ‘iddah ends. (8) A dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) who knows proper washing can perform it, as the sunna is achieved. (9) If a widow gives birth before washing, she may not wash him because the ‘iddah of death ends with delivery. These rulings all hinge on the condition that the marital permissibility (hilliyyah) must exist at the time of washing.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bore a child to her master
مكاتبة (mukataba): A slave woman under a contract of manumission
مدبرة (mudabbara): A slave woman promised freedom after the master's death
ظهار (zihar): A husband declaring his wife like his mother's back, a pre-Islamic divorce practice
شبهة (shubha): Mistaken intercourse (e.g., thinking she is his wife)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man dies leaving a wife who is a Magian (مجوسية). According to the passage, may she wash him?
A) Yes, because marriage is valid
B) No, because it was not permissible for him to touch her during life
C) Yes, if she converts to Islam first
D) No, but she may perform tayammum

Key Takeaway
The permissibility to wash a deceased spouse is contingent upon the existence of a valid marriage at the time of washing, and a husband may never wash his wife.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Impurity of the Deceased Before Washing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity of deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the impurity of the deceased before washing, apply the evidence for each, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
The deceased before washing is najis by death in the relied-upon Hanafi view, not merely hadath.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute: Is the unwashed deceased in a state of hadath (minor impurity) or substantive najasah (ritual impurity)? The answer affects whether the body contaminates water and other surfaces. We'll explore both views, their evidence, and why the majority of Hanafi scholars adopted the najasah view.

Guided Path
1
State the two views
Identify the two positions: (1) The deceased is in hadath only; washing is obligatory to avoid hardship. (2) The deceased becomes najis by death; washing purifies the body.
The first view is introduced with 'قيل' (it is said), the second with 'وقيل' (and it is said).
2
Examine the evidence for each view
For the hadath view: the basis is avoiding hardship (haraj) in pinpointing specific impurities. For the najasah view: evidence includes that the body contaminates water if it falls in and invalidates prayer while being carried.
The najasah evidence is explicitly cited from al-Muhit.
3
Analyze the Tarjih (preponderance)
Al-Kafi declares the najasah view correct, and al-Bada'i' attributes it to the generality of shaykhs. This indicates the mufta bihi position.
The tarjih authority is from later Hanafi works.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the deceased is najis, then water or surfaces contacted before washing become impure. If only hadath, they do not. This affects rulings of taharah.
The ruling matters for a person who touches or carries the unwashed body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكمه قبله ففيه اختلاف؛ فقيل : إنه محدث وهو سبب وجوبه لنجاسة حلت به، وإنما وجب غسل جميع الجسد لعدم الحرج . وقيل : ينجس بالموت واقتصر عليه في المحيط مستدلاً بأنه لو وقع في الماء القليل قبل الغسل نجسه، ولو صلى وهو حامل للميت لا يجوز فيجب تطهيره بالغسل شرعاً كرامة له هـ. وصححه في الكافي ونسبه في البدائع إلى عامة المشايخ .
As for the ruling on him before washing, there is disagreement in it. It is said: He is in a state of hadath, and it is the cause of its obligation due to impurity that has occurred to him, and washing the whole body is required to avoid hardship. And it is said: He becomes impure (najis) by death, and it is restricted to this in al-Muhit, using as evidence that if he falls into little water before washing, he makes it impure; and if one prays while carrying the deceased, it is not permissible, so it is obligatory to purify him by washing, as an honor for him. And it (the second opinion) is declared correct in al-Kafi and attributed in al-Bada'i' to the generality of the shaykhs.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the state of the deceased before it is washed. Two views are cited: The first view (qila) says that the deceased is in a state of minor impurity (hadath), and this impurity is the reason why washing is obligatory. It obligates washing the entire body to avoid hardship (haraj) of pinpointing specific impurities. The second view (qila) asserts that the deceased becomes truly impure (najis) by death itself. This is the view adopted in al-Muhit, supported by evidence: if the unwashed body falls into a small amount of water, it renders the water impure (which would not happen with hadath alone). Also, it is invalid to pray while carrying the unwashed deceased, indicating substantive impurity. The washing purifies the body as an honor (karamah) for the deceased. Al-Kafi declares this view correct, and al-Bada'i' attributes it to the majority of Hanafi scholars (عامة المشايخ). The dispute affects practical matters: if the deceased is considered najis, then the unwashed body contaminates surfaces and requires purification; if only hadath, it does not. The second view (najis bil-mawt) is the stronger and relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to al-Kafi and others. This discussion is a typical 'talil' (identification of effective cause) and has implications for the rules of najasah and taharah.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): A state of minor ritual impurity that necessitates wudu'
نجاسة (najasah): Ritual impurity that prevents prayer and requires physical purification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view adopted in al-Muhit and al-Kafi, what is the state of the deceased before washing?
A) The deceased is in a state of hadath only
B) The deceased is ritually impure (najis) due to death
C) The deceased is pure and needs no washing
D) The deceased is impure only if there are visible contaminants

Key Takeaway
The deceased before washing is considered najis (ritually impure) by the relied-upon Hanafi view; this is supported by the evidence that the body contaminates water and invalidates prayer while carried.

Conditions for Spouses and Masters to Wash the Deceased
States the core ruling on spousal and master-slave washing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a husband may wash his wife, a wife may wash her husband, and a master may wash his female slave.

Core Idea
Spouses may wash each other only if marriage is intact; a master never washes his female slave.

This lesson covers the foundational rulings on who is qualified to perform ghusl for the deceased. The text establishes that the marital bond must be valid at the time of washing, and that a master-slave relationship does not confer the right to wash.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its obligation
Ghusl of the deceased is a communal obligation (fard kifayah). This lesson specifies who among the living may perform it.
2
State the general spousal rule
A wife may wash her husband and a husband may wash his wife, regardless of consummation, as long as the marriage is valid at the time of washing.
The ruling is reciprocal.
3
Identify the condition: continuity of marriage
The marriage must still be intact. If the wife is separated by divorce and in her iddah, or forbidden due to apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, she cannot wash him.
Apostasy severs the marriage instantly.
4
State the master-slave rule
A master does not wash his umm walad, mudabbara, or mukataba. Conversely, they do not wash him. This is the well-known position of Abu Hanifah.
This is due to the lack of a lawful relationship permitting touch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنائز والصبي الذي لا يشتهى والصبية كذلك غسلهما الرجال والنساء، ولا يغسل الرجل زوجته والزوجة تغسل زوجها دخل بها أو لا بشرط بقاء الزوجية عند الغسل حتى لو كانت مبانة بالطلاق وهي في العدة أو محرمة بردة أو رضاع أو مصاهرة لم تغسله، ولم يغسل المولى أم ولده وكذا مدبرته ومكاتبته وكذا على العكس في المشهور عن أبي حنيفة. الكل في المجتبى . وفي الواقعات : رجل له امرأتان قال إحداكما طالق ثلاثاً بعد الدخول بهما ثم مات قبل أن يبين فليس لواحدة منهما أن تغسله الجواز أن كل واحدة منهما ،مطلقة ولهما الميراث وعليهما عدة الطلاق والوفاة. ولو مات عن امرأته وهي مجوسية لم تغسله لأنه كان لا يحل له المس حال حياته فكذا بعد وفاته بخلاف التي ظاهر منها لأن الحل قائم، فإن أسلمت قبل أن يغسل غسلته اعتباراً بحالة الحياة. وكذا لو مات عن امرأته وأختها منه في عدته لم تغسله فإن انقضت عدتها قبل أن يغسل غسلته لما قلنا ا هـ . وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتدت المنكوحة بعد موته أو قبلت ابنه لا تغسله وكذا إذا وطئت بالشبهة لأن هذه الأشياء تنافي النكاح وتحرم المس. وفيها : إذا كان مع النساء رجل من أهل الذمة أو مع الرحال امرأة ذمية يعلمان الغسل لأن السنة تتأدى بغسله ولكن لا يهتدى إلى السنة فيعلم. وفي المحيط : لو مات عنها وهي حامل فوضعت لا تغسله لانقضاء عدتها.
Book of Funerals. A boy who is not desired (sexually) and a girl likewise: their washing is done by men and women. A man does not wash his wife, and the wife washes her husband, whether he consummated or not, on condition that the marriage continues at the time of washing. So if she is separated by divorce while in iddah, or forbidden by apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, she does not wash him. A master does not wash his umm walad, nor his mudabbara, nor his mukataba, and similarly the opposite according to the well-known from Abu Hanifah. All of this is in al-Mujtaba. In al-Waqi'at: A man had two wives and said, 'One of you is thrice divorced' after consummation with both, then he died before specifying. Then neither of them may wash him because each of them is divorced. They both inherit and must observe the iddah of divorce and death. If a man dies leaving his wife a Magian, she does not wash him because it was not permissible for him to touch her during his life, similarly after his death, unlike one from whom he had performed dhihar because permissibility remains. If she converts before he is washed, she washes him, considering the state of life. Similarly if he dies leaving his wife and her sister (from him) in her iddah, she does not wash him. If her iddah expires before he is washed, she washes him, because of what we said. In al-Walwaljiyya: If the wife apostatizes after his death, or she kisses his son, she does not wash him. Similarly if she is penetrated in a dubious contract, because these things contradict marriage and forbid touching. In it: If among women there is a dhimmi man, or among men a dhimmi woman who knows how to wash, because the Sunnah is achieved by his washing, but he is not guided to the Sunnah so he is taught. In al-Muhit: If a woman dies pregnant and delivers, she does not wash him because her iddah has expired.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding who is qualified or obligated to perform the ritual washing (ghusl) of a deceased person. The core rule is that a husband may wash his wife and vice versa, provided the marriage is intact at the time of washing. If the marriage has been dissolved by divorce (especially during iddah), apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, the right to wash is forfeited. Additionally, a master does not wash his female slaves (umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba), and conversely, they do not wash him, as stated by Abu Hanifah. The text then presents several specific cases: a man with two wives who pronounces a triple divorce ambiguously before death; a Magian wife; a wife who apostatizes; a case of dubious intercourse; and the presence of dhimmis as washers. Each case reinforces the principle that legal permissibility of physical contact at the time of washing determines the right to perform ghusl. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Muhit that a pregnant woman who delivers before washing her husband may not wash him because her iddah has expired.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): The ritual washing of the entire body of the deceased.
ميت (mayyit): The deceased person requiring washing and shrouding.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which marriage is not permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The right to wash a deceased spouse depends on the marriage being legally valid at the moment of death, and no master-slave washing is permitted.

Edge Cases in Ghusl: Ambiguous Divorce, Non-Muslims, and Apostasy
Presents specific cases illustrating the application of the spousal washing rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of continuous marriage to special scenarios including ambiguous divorce, non-Muslim spouses, and apostasy, and explain the reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
The right to wash hinges on legal permissibility of touch at death, which is determined by the state of the marriage or the washer’s faith.

This lesson explores specific scenarios that test the boundaries of the spousal washing rule. Through cases of ambiguous divorce, Magian wives, apostasy, and dhimmis, the text clarifies that the legal permissibility of physical contact at the moment of death determines the right to perform ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the ambiguous divorce case
A man with two wives says 'One of you is thrice divorced' (without specifying) and dies. Neither wife can wash him because each is potentially divorced, so the marriage is not certain for either.
Both inherit and observe iddah, but the right to wash is lost due to uncertainty.
2
Examine the Magian wife case
A Magian (non-Muslim) wife cannot wash her Muslim husband because touching her was impermissible during his life. Compare with a wife from whom he had performed dhihar, where permissibility remains.
If she converts before he is washed, she may wash him because permissibility is restored.
3
Apply the sister-in-law case
If a man dies leaving his wife and her sister (who is also his wife? Actually 'wa ukhtiha minhu' means her sister from his side? The text says if he dies leaving his wife and her sister from him in her iddah, she does not wash him. If the iddah expires, she may wash him.
This reinforces that iddah from a previous marriage can prevent washing, but when it ends, the right returns.
4
Evaluate the apostasy and dubious intercourse cases
If the wife apostatizes after his death, or she kisses his son (implying a relationship that makes marriage unlawful?), or if she had intercourse under a dubious contract, she cannot wash him because these actions nullify the marriage or forbid touching.
The text emphasizes that these things 'tanafi al-nikah wa tuharrim al-mass'.
5
Consider the dhimmi washer case
If a dhimmi man is present among women, or a dhimmi woman among men, and he/she knows how to wash, they may perform the ghusl because the Sunnah is achieved by their washing. They should be taught the proper method.
This shows that the washer being Muslim is not a condition; knowledge of the Sunnah is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنائز والصبي الذي لا يشتهى والصبية كذلك غسلهما الرجال والنساء، ولا يغسل الرجل زوجته والزوجة تغسل زوجها دخل بها أو لا بشرط بقاء الزوجية عند الغسل حتى لو كانت مبانة بالطلاق وهي في العدة أو محرمة بردة أو رضاع أو مصاهرة لم تغسله، ولم يغسل المولى أم ولده وكذا مدبرته ومكاتبته وكذا على العكس في المشهور عن أبي حنيفة. الكل في المجتبى . وفي الواقعات : رجل له امرأتان قال إحداكما طالق ثلاثاً بعد الدخول بهما ثم مات قبل أن يبين فليس لواحدة منهما أن تغسله الجواز أن كل واحدة منهما ،مطلقة ولهما الميراث وعليهما عدة الطلاق والوفاة. ولو مات عن امرأته وهي مجوسية لم تغسله لأنه كان لا يحل له المس حال حياته فكذا بعد وفاته بخلاف التي ظاهر منها لأن الحل قائم، فإن أسلمت قبل أن يغسل غسلته اعتباراً بحالة الحياة. وكذا لو مات عن امرأته وأختها منه في عدته لم تغسله فإن انقضت عدتها قبل أن يغسل غسلته لما قلنا ا هـ . وفي الولوالجية : إذا ارتدت المنكوحة بعد موته أو قبلت ابنه لا تغسله وكذا إذا وطئت بالشبهة لأن هذه الأشياء تنافي النكاح وتحرم المس. وفيها : إذا كان مع النساء رجل من أهل الذمة أو مع الرحال امرأة ذمية يعلمان الغسل لأن السنة تتأدى بغسله ولكن لا يهتدى إلى السنة فيعلم. وفي المحيط : لو مات عنها وهي حامل فوضعت لا تغسله لانقضاء عدتها.
Book of Funerals. A boy who is not desired (sexually) and a girl likewise: their washing is done by men and women. A man does not wash his wife, and the wife washes her husband, whether he consummated or not, on condition that the marriage continues at the time of washing. So if she is separated by divorce while in iddah, or forbidden by apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, she does not wash him. A master does not wash his umm walad, nor his mudabbara, nor his mukataba, and similarly the opposite according to the well-known from Abu Hanifah. All of this is in al-Mujtaba. In al-Waqi'at: A man had two wives and said, 'One of you is thrice divorced' after consummation with both, then he died before specifying. Then neither of them may wash him because each of them is divorced. They both inherit and must observe the iddah of divorce and death. If a man dies leaving his wife a Magian, she does not wash him because it was not permissible for him to touch her during his life, similarly after his death, unlike one from whom he had performed dhihar because permissibility remains. If she converts before he is washed, she washes him, considering the state of life. Similarly if he dies leaving his wife and her sister (from him) in her iddah, she does not wash him. If her iddah expires before he is washed, she washes him, because of what we said. In al-Walwaljiyya: If the wife apostatizes after his death, or she kisses his son, she does not wash him. Similarly if she is penetrated in a dubious contract, because these things contradict marriage and forbid touching. In it: If among women there is a dhimmi man, or among men a dhimmi woman who knows how to wash, because the Sunnah is achieved by his washing, but he is not guided to the Sunnah so he is taught. In al-Muhit: If a woman dies pregnant and delivers, she does not wash him because her iddah has expired.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding who is qualified or obligated to perform the ritual washing (ghusl) of a deceased person. The core rule is that a husband may wash his wife and vice versa, provided the marriage is intact at the time of washing. If the marriage has been dissolved by divorce (especially during iddah), apostasy, fosterage, or affinity, the right to wash is forfeited. Additionally, a master does not wash his female slaves (umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba), and conversely, they do not wash him, as stated by Abu Hanifah. The text then presents several specific cases: a man with two wives who pronounces a triple divorce ambiguously before death; a Magian wife; a wife who apostatizes; a case of dubious intercourse; and the presence of dhimmis as washers. Each case reinforces the principle that legal permissibility of physical contact at the time of washing determines the right to perform ghusl. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Muhit that a pregnant woman who delivers before washing her husband may not wash him because her iddah has expired.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): The ritual washing of the entire body of the deceased.
ميت (mayyit): The deceased person requiring washing and shrouding.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which marriage is not permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Recommended Qualities of the Washer and His Ruling
States preferences for the washer and whether the washer becomes impure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended qualities of the washer (ghasil) and identify whether the washer must perform ghusl or wudu after washing the deceased.

Core Idea
The best washer is the closest relative, and no ghusl or wudu is required after washing the deceased.

This passage from al-Mujtaba provides guidance on who should ideally perform the ritual washing of the deceased and addresses common questions about the washer's state of purity. Understanding these recommendations and the ruling that washing the deceased does not nullify ritual purity is essential for those involved in funeral rites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its ruling
The passage discusses the person who washes the deceased (ghasil). The act of choosing a washer is not obligatory but recommended (sunnah ghayr muakkadah).
The ruling on the washer's identity is one of preference, not obligation.
2
State the preferred qualities
The best washer is the closest relative of the deceased. If that person lacks knowledge of the proper washing procedure, then someone known for trustworthiness (amanah) and piety (wara') should be chosen.
The hadith reference supports this recommendation.
3
Note the permissibility exceptions
The washer may be in a state of janabah, menstruation, or even be a disbeliever; all are permissible, though a Jewish or Christian woman washing her husband is more disliked (aqbah).
This shows flexibility in need.
4
Conclude with the purity ruling
After washing the deceased, the washer is not required to perform ghusl or wudu. The act itself does not nullify ritual purity.
This contradicts a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى وأما ما يستحب للغاسل فالأولى أن يكون أقرب الناس إلى الميت فإن لم يعلم الغسل فأهل الأمانة والورع للحديث، فإن كان الغاسل جنباً أو حائضاً أو كافراً جاز، واليهودية والنصرانية كالمسلمة في غسل زوجها لكنه أقبح، وليس على من غسل ميتاً غسل ولا وضوء ا هـ .
In al-Mujtaba: As for what is recommended for the washer: the best is that he be the closest relative to the deceased. If he does not know how to wash, then people of trustworthiness and piety, due to the hadith. If the washer is in a state of janabah, menstruation, or is a disbeliever, it is permissible. A Jewish or Christian woman is like a Muslim woman in washing her husband, but it is more disliked. And there is no ghusl nor wudu upon the one who washes a deceased person.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage from al-Mujtaba outlines the recommended characteristics of the person who performs the ritual washing (ghusl) of the deceased. Ideally, the washer should be the closest relative to the deceased, but if that person lacks knowledge of the proper procedure, then someone known for trustworthiness and piety should be chosen. The text then addresses the permissibility of a washer in a state of major impurity (janabah, menstruation) or even a disbeliever—it is valid but less ideal. Specifically, a Jewish or Christian woman may wash her husband, though it is disliked. Finally, a crucial ruling: the washer himself is not required to perform ghusl or wudu after washing the deceased, meaning that washing a dead body does not nullify one's own ritual purity.

Key Terms
غاسل (ghasil): The person who performs the ritual washing of the deceased.
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
حائض (ha'id): Menstruating woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A Jewish woman may wash her Muslim husband.

Key Takeaway
The washer of a deceased Muslim should ideally be a close relative or a trustworthy pious person, and after washing, the washer retains his or her existing state of ritual purity without needing additional ghusl or wudu.

Legal Status of the Deceased Before Washing: Najis or Hadath?
Internal Hanafi dispute on the deceased's ritual status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the legal status of the deceased before washing and explain the evidence for the preferred view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school is divided: is the deceased's body najis or simply in hadath? The preferred view is najis, but a hadith raises the question.

In this lesson, we examine an internal Hanafi debate: what is the state of a Muslim's body immediately after death before it is washed? Is it ritually impure (najis) or in a state of minor impurity (hadath)? The answer affects rulings such as the validity of prayer while carrying the body and whether the deceased can be touched without wudu. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents both views and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the disputed ruling
What exactly is the legal status of the deceased before washing? Identify the precise point of ikhtilaf.
2
List the two positions and their proponents
One view says the body is in hadath; the other says it becomes najis. Note that the text attributes the hadath view to 'qil' (an unnamed source) and the najis view to al-Muhit and the majority.
3
Examine the practical evidence for najasah
The author cites two practial implications: if the deceased falls into low water, it becomes impure; and praying while carrying the body invalidates the prayer. These indicate najasah.
4
Evaluate the counter-dalil
The hadith of Abu Hurairah (r) negates impurity for the deceased. The author notes that if authentic, it would support the hadath view, but the majority accepts the najasah view anyway.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Al-Kafi declared the najasah view correct, and al-Bada'i attributed it to the majority of mashayikh. This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكمه قبله ففيه اختلاف؛ فقيل : إنه محدث وهو سبب وجوبه لنجاسة حلت به، وإنما وجب غسل جميع الجسد لعدم الحرج . وقيل : ينجس بالموت واقتصر عليه في المحيط مستدلاً بأنه لو وقع في الماء القليل قبل الغسل نجسه، ولو صلى وهو حامل للميت لا يجوز فيجب تطهيره بالغسل شرعاً كرامة له هـ. وصححه في الكافي ونسبه في البدائع إلى عامة المشايخ . قال في فتح القدير : وشرفاً ا. وقد روي في حديث أبي هريرة سبحان الله إن الميت لا ينجس حياً ولا ميتاً»(۱) فإن صحت وجب ترجيح أنها للحدث ا.هـ. واتفقوا أن حكمه بعده إن كان مسلماً الطهارة ولذا يصلى عليه، فما يتوهم من أن الحنفية إنما منعوا من الصلاة عليه في المسجد لأجل نجساته خطأ، واتفقوا على أن الكافر لا يطهر بالغسل وأنه لا تصح صلاة حامله بعده .
As for his ruling before it (i.e., before washing), there is disagreement. It is said that he is in a state of hadath (minor impurity), and that is the cause of its obligation due to impurity that occurred to him, and the washing of the entire body is obligatory to avoid hardship. And it is said that he becomes najis by death, and this was relied upon in al-Muhit, arguing that if he falls into a small amount of water before washing, he makes it impure, and if one prays while carrying the deceased, it is not valid, so it is obligatory to purify him by ghusl as a shara'i honor. Al-Kafi declared this correct and al-Bada'i attributed it to the majority of mashayikh. In Fath al-Qadir, he said 'wa sharafan' [and honor]. And it has been narrated in the hadith of Abu Hurairah, 'Subhanallah, the deceased does not become impure alive nor dead.' If authentic, it necessitates preference that it is for hadath. They agreed that his ruling after it, if he is Muslim, is taharah, and thus prayer is performed upon him. So the misunderstanding that the Hanafis prevent prayer upon him in the mosque due to his impurity is wrong. And they agreed that the disbeliever is not purified by ghusl and that prayer upon the carrier of him afterwards is not valid.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses an internal Hanafi debate about the legal status of the deceased person's body before it is washed. The first view states that the dead body is in a state of hadath (like minor impurity), which necessitates ghusl. The second view holds that the body becomes inherently najis (impure) upon death, as argued in al-Muhit. Evidence for the latter includes: if the deceased falls into a small amount of water, the water becomes impure, and if someone prays while carrying the body, the prayer is invalid. The author of al-Kafi declares the second view correct and ascribes it to most scholars. A hadith from Abu Hurairah ('The deceased does not become impure') is cited, and if authentic, it would support the hadath view. The passage concludes that all agree a Muslim is purified after ghusl, hence prayer is valid, rebutting the claim that Hanafis prevent prayer in the mosque due to impurity. A disbeliever is not purified by ghusl.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): A state of minor ritual impurity requiring wudu or ghusl.
نجاسة (najasah): Ritual impurity that contaminates the body or clothing and must be removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the view that the deceased becomes najis by death?
A) The hadith of Abu Hurairah.
B) If the deceased falls into a small amount of water, it makes it impure.
C) Consensus among all scholars.
D) The impossibility of removing hadath after death.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers the view that the deceased becomes najis by death, but there is a minority view that it is only hadath; after ghusl, all agree the body is pure.
